       "headline": "Iceland contains virus with voluntary app use",
       "articleBody": "&lt;p&gt;Following an extensive programme of testing and tracing, including through voluntary data collection via an App, Iceland has suppressed the spread of the new virus, reporting just 99 active cases on the last day of April.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In total, Iceland has seen 1,798 COVID-19 cases and ten deaths.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Iceland&amp;apos;s success is partly testament to its tiny population: it has a population of just 360,000. But it also reflects decisive action by authorities, who used a rigorous policy of testing and tracing to find and isolate infected people, even when they had no symptoms.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over six weeks, Iceland managed to test almost 50,000 people, more than 13 percent of the population, the biggest chunk of any country in the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That has helped Iceland weather the pandemic without resorting to the near-total social and economic shutdowns enforced in many other European countries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Alongside the testing, civil defense authorities set up a contact tracing team, and a mobile phone tracing app was up and running a few weeks later.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One third of the population have installed the app to date, a fact the contact tracing team attributes to a careful consideration of privacy-related issues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Starting on Monday, gatherings of up to 50 will be permitted, high schools and colleges can resume classes and all businesses except bars, gyms and swimming pools can reopen.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&amp;quot;The app will be important now after 4 May when we will reduce the gathering ban and the restrictions so we are prepared that the pandemic might rise again a little,&amp;quot; said Aevar Palmi Palmason, head of the contact tracing team.&lt;/p&gt;",

href="#post=0e9974f7-2a2c-4b6a-a802-3443385ac4dc" class="title">Media rights group warns coronavirus being used to curb press</a></div><div class="entry__author"><div class="author__profile"><span class="text--author">By <!-- -->Associated Press</span><time class="text--time">03 May 2020 14:19</time></div></div><div><p>An international media rights group says the coronavirus pandemic is being used by governments around the world to increase restrictions on press freedoms.</p></div><div><p>The International Press Institute issued a report Sunday to coincide with World Press Freedom Day 2020, which concludes that in both democratic and autocratic states the &quot;public health crisis has allowed governments to exercise control over the media on the pretext of preventing the spread of disinformation.&quot;</p></div><div class="tweet" data-user="9NewsAUS" data-tweet-id="1256934496978571265"></div><div><p>The Vienna-based organisation said it has documented 162 press freedom violations related to coronavirus coverage over the past two and a half months. Almost a third of the violations have involved the arrest, detention or charging of journalists.</p></div><div class="live-blog__share"><ul data-circles="true" data-count="3" class="sc-bZQynM nights for those in need.</p></div><div><p>So far, they&apos;ve given away 818.</p></div><div><p>&quot;It&apos;s reaching out to the community, it&apos;s helping them who have lost their jobs,&quot; Australia on a mercy flight from India are set to finally go home, and they couldn&apos;t be happier.</p></div><div><p>Balaji and his young family left for a family visit to India in November last year.</p></div><div><p>&quot;I went there for probably about two months, and I got stuck for about five months,&quot; he said.</p></div><div><p>Tomorrow morning, they can finally head home to Melbourne.</p></div><div><p>&quot;First thing is eat, sleep, and just take it easy for probably a couple of days,&quot; he said.</p></div><div><p>But while there&apos;s no place like home, the guests bottom of my heart.&quot;</p></div><div><p>The state government provided gift hampers for children stuck in the Pullman and the Playford hotels.</p></div><div><p>The hotels ran fitness classes several times a day through in-room TVs &#x2013; a good balance to the hearty provisions provided.</p></div><div><p>&quot;We&apos;ve got three meals a day, and those meals have just been so big,&quot; Pullman guest Perrie Kap said.</p></div><div><p>&quot;I think I&apos;ve put on what&apos;s called the &apos;quarantine 15&apos; just from these two weeks staying here.&quot;</p></div><div><p>Pullman Hotel But it also reflects decisive action by authorities, who used a rigorous policy of testing and tracing to find and isolate infected people, even when they had no symptoms.</p><p>Over six weeks, Iceland managed to test almost 50,000 people, more than 13 percent of the population, the biggest chunk of any country in the world.</p><p>That has helped Iceland weather the pandemic without resorting to the near-total social and economic shutdowns enforced in many other European countries.</p><p>Alongside the testing, civil defense authorities set up a contact tracing team, and a mobile phone tracing app was up and running a few weeks later.</p><p>One third of the population have installed the app to date, a fact the contact tracing team attributes to a careful consideration of privacy-related issues.</p><p>Starting on Monday, gatherings of up to 50 will be permitted, high schools and colleges can resume classes and all businesses except bars, gyms and swimming pools can reopen.</p><p>&quot;The app will be important now after 4 May when we will reduce the gathering ban and the restrictions so we are prepared that the pandemic might rise again a little,&quot; said Aevar Palmi Palmason, head of the contact tracing team.</p>","multiMedia":["<p>Following an extensive programme of testing and tracing, including through voluntary data collection via an App, Iceland has suppressed the spread of the new virus, reporting just 99 active cases on the last day of April.</p>","<p>In total, Iceland has seen 1,798 COVID-19 cases and ten deaths.</p>","<p>Iceland&apos;s success is partly testament to its tiny population: it has a population of just 360,000.
